ix
==PAGE:10==
==PAGE:11==
Summary (in english)
The main objective of the project is to develop an eﬃcient and eﬀective method to solve a quadratic
problem using a platform independent programming language. This is achieved using C as the main
programming language to achieve code that reﬂects both eﬃciency and platform independence.
A basis for eﬀectiveness is established by utilization of the Active set method and applying Null
space methods to solve inequality constrained problems. This approach is then extended to include
updating methods and thereby achieve faster run times. The basis implementation relies on full
factorizations for each iteration of the active set.
The methods employed for execution of the proposed methods include Givens rotations and
Householder transformations to produce QR factorizations and zeroing needed when updating both
QR and LDL′ factorizations included in the Null Space solver. Both methods are theoretically
analyzed to establish complexity of operations needed for successful completion and then compared
to their corresponding update methods. The theoretical results are compared to the results seen
when implementations are run and discussed. Furthermore, run times for all methods are compared
to ensure the expected beneﬁts are realized.
Results for the Active Set method are then gathered and concluded upon and the desired increase
of iteration and convergence speed is achieved. These results are discussed together with all the
elements leading to the completed solver, and opportunities for future developments are presented.
The tests and comparison show that the updating methods for the Active Set method signiﬁcantly
speed up the convergence speed while retaining accuracy. Furthermore, that all methods applied
within the thesis are functional and return correct results within machine precision.
The thesis reviews some of the implications of choosing a lower level language like C and justify
choices made during development.
xi
==PAGE:12==
==PAGE:13==
Sammenfating (Summery)1
Denne opgave har til hovedformål at udvikle en fuld funktionelt Active Set metode til løsning af
anden grads modeller ved brug af et programmerings sprog som er platform uafhængig. Programme-
ringssproget C derfor valgt. Grundet i at C både er hurtigt og eﬀektivt i kørsel. For sammenligning
benyttes en basal metode af Active Set baseret på Null Space med komplette faktoriseringer i
hvert skridt. Den basale fremgangsmåde udvides med udledninger der opdatere QR og LDL′
faktoriseringerne hvilke indgår i Null Space metoden.
Teorien der benyttes i denne afhandling inkluderer Givens rotationer og Householder reﬂeksioner til
beregning af QR faktoriseringen samt udledning af opdateringer for både QR og LDL faktoriseringer.
Alle anvendelser er analyseret for sammenligning af kompleksitet af operationer der udføres under
faktoriseringer.
De anvendte opdaterings metoder er herefter sammenholdt og vurderet. Disse analyser er holdt op
imod resultaterne fra kørsler af programmeret metode. Desuden er processtiderne sammenlignet for
at de eftervise at forventede fordele er realiseret.
Resultaterne for Active Set metoden er samlet og konklusioner drages heraf hvorvidt den ønsket
eﬀektivisering er opnået. Disse konklusioner samt alle resulter af anvendte metoder er diskuteret.
Forslag til fremtidige udvidelser blive præsenteret her efter.
Speciale opgaven viser at opdaterings teorien der benyttes i Active Set metoden øger hastiheden af
konvergering, samt bibeholder præcision af løsningen. Derudover eftervises det at alle metoder der
benyttes er funktionelle og returnerer resultater indenfor maskinens præcision.
Dette speciale opgave behandler også følsomheden overfor når et lower level language som C vælges.

skrives i kursiv.

Chapter 1
Introduction
1.1
Introduction and Motivation
Motivation. The motivation behind this master thesis is to establish a deeper understanding of
all aspects that are necessary to develop a complete mathematical quadratic problem (QP) solver.
This is based on understanding market leading technologies and implementing them to the solver.
Before the master thesis start my courses have focused on developing mathematical models and
proof of concept methods when implementing algorithms, where MATLAB or Python have been
used. The main objective of this thesis is to implement a complete and eﬃcient convex Quadratic
Problem solver and obtain a deeper understanding of the mathematics and computations carried
out to solve this problem. In addition earning C programming and exploring C’s capabilities as
this is a corner stone of the scientiﬁc computing community and is essential for development of
fast methods. This motivates the usage of C as the main programming language and also the
development of skills within a central computer language for scientiﬁc computing.
The subject of this master thesis is Convex Quadratic Programming Algorithms and Software and
will contribute by developing an open source QP solver that is completely platform independent as
none currently exist.
1.2
Main Objectives and Contributions
We are faced with increased global exposure in modern society resulting in competition on all fronts
of any process. Survival in this environment requires companies to achieve a competitive advantage
on key aspects with emphasis on producing smarter at reduced cost and cost reduction to retain a
competitive advantage and proﬁtability. This requires improved quality and increased productivity
while maintaining high margins. This double edged sword calls for better products at lower cost
and thereby raising the need for minimizing resource usage whilst retaining or increasing asset
performance.
Many modern systems rely on optimization as a key aspect of the production line to solve these
demanding tasks and to control production. Countless technologies are based on small and/or
embedded systems where much of the software must be as self contained as possible. Yet today
current optimization libraries are often based on linear Algebra packages similar to LAPACK,
a library based on FORTRAN, that makes compiling and running implementations diﬃcult on
standalone appliances.
1
==PAGE:28==
1.
Introduction
Compiling diﬃculties arise as result of utilizing existing implementations to strengthen and simplify
development as LAPACK and similar libraries have a proven track record and are often presumed
to be "best in class". The outcome of this results in very few competing implementations that
are open source which do not rely on FORTRAN back-end to compute results. Therefore this
contributes to diﬃculties in implementing optimization in key areas of a production. The objective
of this master thesis is to produce a working implementation of state of the art methods that can
be used in any environment.
This target will be achieved by implementing current working theory such that a baseline can be
established for further investigation into more optimal methods. This includes implementation of
algorithms that are currently well described in literature. Then these will be tested against more
eﬃcient methods. Emphasis will be put the implementation of theory and extensive work will be
done in coding and testing, ensuring stability of the solver.
All implementation will be done in c99 using no libraries thus resulting in a stand alone product
that will be able to run on most systems with few if any tweaks.
In this master thesis, theory needed to apply a basic version of the primal active set algorithm
and an optimized version using updating techniques to increase the eﬃciency of solving is covered.
Implementation speciﬁc considerations are discussed and compared to ensure stability and eﬃciency
of the program.
1.3
Outline of the Thesis
Chapter 2. handles literature review and an introduction into the baseline theory of the project
(KKT conditions and convexity) thus establishing the foundation for the rest of the project.
Chapter 3. introduces the methods needed to utilize the factorizations to solve systems of linear
equations. These will also be gathered into algorithms that will then solve the problems that are
presented in the thesis.
Chapter 4. introduces the formal deﬁnition of the Active Set method and its methods.
Chapter 5. details the linear algebra and computations needed for the proposed extensions of
the project. It also highlights few considerations needed when working with scientiﬁc computation
that underline the challenges when implementing theory into working programs and considerations
needed to run more optimal code. Chapter 5 also introduces all the factorization methods and
theory needed for the future work and updating strategies.
Chapter 6. introduces the specialization done to the Active Set method to incorporate the
updating methods and shortly discusses the implications of adding updating methods.
Chapter 7. discusses the programming implications and justiﬁes the usage of data types. This
chapter also discusses the beneﬁts of the diﬀerent programming languages C, MATLAB and Python.
Chapter 8. tests all methods with focus on the errors and run time of the implementations.
Thoroughly documenting implications of size on both errors and run time. All results are displayed
in most insightful graph type to give a visual representation. Tests focus on assuring errors are
minimal but run time are recorded simultaneously to compare with theoretical analysis. Additionally
presents the ﬁndings of the tests run on the implementations of the Active Set method.

==PAGE:30==
1.
Introduction
1.5
Implementation concerns
When implementing the solver a consideration is to adhere to the current prevailing structure found
in solvers. This is the column major structure that originates from the FORTRAN programming
language. Therefore this data structure is implemented for all outer most functions, but internally
everything is handled in row major, following the data structure of the programming language C.
Column major was not found to be more beneﬁcial to use in run time or error handling, and thus
row major was chosen to conform with c99 standards.

2.1.1
Inequality Constrained QP Solvers
Alternative methods for solving QP is introduced in the following and discussion of the beneﬁts the
primal Active Set method poses and is compared to others. These methods are described in [35].
– Primal Active Set
– Dual Active Set
– Interior Point(IP) algorithms
All three methods poses the possibility to solve a QP problem where primal and dual Active Set
algorithms utilize the same fundamental theory but solve the primal or dual problem respectively.
Where IP solvers operate by traversing the interior of the feasible space. The primal Active Set
method poses the possibility to be warm started, meaning in the situation that approximate
1Within the framework of the Active Set method an active set also exists, therefor to diﬀerentiate between the
method and the set, the method is capitalized as "Active Set"
6
==PAGE:33==
2.2.
Literature Review
solutions are known to be feasible these can be inserted at starting points and thereby increase
convergence time of the algorithm. This property is also present in the dual method but ﬁnding a
more optimal starting location that remains dual feasible can be more diﬃcult, yet where the primal
needs a primal feasible starting point, the solution to the unconstrained optimization problem is
an accepted starting point for the dual problem. Both methods generate a sequence of iterates
that are feasible within there respective problem formulation. The primal method then maintains
feasibility of the primal inequalities while improving the objective function that corresponds to
diminishing the dual variables. In contrast the dual maintains feasibility in the dual space while
increasing feasibility step wise to the primal conditions.
For the IP methods feasibility is also maintained for each problem formulation but compared to
iteratively moving on the edges or vertices of the feasible space the IP method moves within the
interior of the feasible space and iteratively improve the solution.
In situations of few degrees of freedom and/or warm start possibilities it is seen that the Active Set
methods dominate in convergence rate. The Active Set methods can though resolve to become
NP when the constraint matrix is very large. This pitfall is not observed in the IP algorithm
and further more it is found that the number of iterations for IP methods converge towards ﬁnite
number of iterations, given any problem size [35].
2.2
Literature Review
Within Quadratic optimization two prevailing methods to implement the optimization scheme
exists, the Active Set method and the IP method. Both methods are found to be eﬀective at solving
the general quadratic problem, and in general cases can be used interchangeably.
The Active Set method employs methods described by [16, 17, 19] and iteratively improve and
reﬁne the solution and direction. The described method by [16] is called the primal Active Set
method and utilized the same theoretical background as the Simplex method for linear programs.
The Active Set method is seen to have strengths when the degrees of freedom are small (dimensions
of the problem constraints/variables are similar) and can move with large step sizes within each
iteration.
Dual primal Active Set methods also exist and use the same baseline theory as the primal method.
The dual method is described by [22] and is shown to be beneﬁcial when a primal feasible point
is not known beforehand, as this method initially solves the unrestricted problem and iteratively
moves into the primal feasible space by adding and conforming to constraints from the primal
space.
Within Philip E. Gill’s description updating techniques from [17] for reducing the amount of
computations needed to apply the Active Set method are employed. These methods use the Null
space method with QR and LDL′ factorization to solve the problem where beforehand similar
solution methods needed re-computation of all factorization in each step. Further [25] discusses
the QR updating in a very descriptive fashion. Describing the methods needed to update in an
algorithmic setting, and [17] details both update techniques in great mathematical detail. The
current software QPOPT[21] implements this method with FORTRAN as the chosen language.
QPOPT proposed method assumes a dense problem formulation and is not suited for problems
where underlying structure of the program can be used. This includes a speciﬁc sparsity pattern,
as would be seen in Model predictive control (MPC), where the formulation often is block diagonal.
7
==PAGE:34==
2.
Preliminaries
Further development of this method to add a Schur complement method to handle some underlying
structure of MPC is discussed in the work of [28] that implements methods from [37].
[22] has then shown that the Dual Active Set algorithm is an eﬃcient and stable method based on
the same theory as the primal Active Set method seen in [16, 17, 19], with modiﬁcations to the
problem formulation. That is the dual formulation is used in the dual Active Set method. This
Method builds on the PhD dissertation [26] that introduces the dual method for solving quadratic
problems. [22] concludes that the dual method is beneﬁcial when an initial feasible point is not
known beforehand.
[22] also shortly discusses issues regarding degeneracy and rounding errors. This aspect is also
discussed by [41, 42] where handling of round oﬀerrors in ill conditioned situations are tested as
well.
To avoid speciﬁc errors in computation while keeping high performance in non-parallel environment
[4] discuss and compares methods to implement mixed precision methods, and thus maintaining a
high level of accuracy from double precision, and speed from single precision. Further papers of
numerical errors are discussed by [38, 41, 42, 44] and performance considerations by [34].
Initially, when John Von Neumann introduced the iIP method it was found not to be eﬃcient in
practice, and has since Von Neumann been reﬁned to [33] that now provides the basis for most IP
methods, within both linear and quadratic programming. The IP method uses a predictor and
corrector step to remain within the interior of the feasible space. This method solves a ﬁxed size
of equations and thus also results in a similar iteration time. IP method have also been found to
be eﬃcient when solving QP and have also been extensively discussed in the scientiﬁc community
[13, 31, 43, 45] for both general QP and MPC. Additionally [6] compares IP methods to the Active
Set methods for MPC. [6] shows the IP method converges in similar number of steps for diﬀerent
sizes of problems but the cpu time increases as the complexity of each iteration is increased. The IP
method is then compared to QPOPT and another Active Set method applying Schur complement
methods. There it is seen that the IP method converges faster when the problem is small (10
Output horizon) but is slower when the problem is of medium size (20-40 Output horizon) and
similar in running time when large (80 Output horizon ) compared to both Active Set methods.
These papers show that solving the QP is not an arbitrary task and diﬀerent methods poses
strengths that make them better suited to speciﬁc problems. This is to be kept in mind when
choosing one method over the other. In this thesis the focus will be on the primal Active Set
method for a general dense system and will be based the methods proposed by [16]. Considerations
by [38, 41, 42, 44] are also discussed to ensure stability of the solution.
8

Null Space Solver

3.1.
Equality Constrained QP Solvers
3.1.2
LDL′ Solver
The LDL′ solver is one of the fastest methods to solve the system of linear equations Ax = b, with
the pitfall of requiring the A matrix to be semideﬁnite. This is a result of the LDL′ factorization
failing when the A matrix is non-semideﬁnite. The method of solving the equation system is as
follows.
Lemma 3.1.1. Given a positive semideﬁnite matrix A and a vector b the solution to an equation
system Ax = b, where x is the solution matrix, can be found using the LDL′ factorization of
A = LDL′ by solving,
Ly = b
(3.9)
DL′x = y
(3.10)
Remark 2. To solve the two equations (3.9) and (3.10) can be done by forward and backward
substitution for L and L′. When multiplying D the diagonal nature of D can be used to avoid
multiplying by many zeros and thereby reducing the amount of computations needed to resolve
(3.10). The algorithms for forward and backward substitution are put in the appendix B.1
The previous lemma is now formalized into an algorithm to simplify the implementation
Algorithm 2 LDL′ Solver
1: Construct KKT matrix from H,A from 4.9 and RHS (d′ = −[g′, b′])
2: LDL′ factorize KKT matrix into lower L, diagonal vector D a
3: Forward substitute to solve Ly = d
4: Divide y with vector D to solve (y = y/D)
5: Backward substitute to solve L′z = y
6: Solution is found to be z.
The LDL′ solver is proposed to solve the equation system within the Null space solver i.e. equation
(3.6). Further it can also be used as an alternative method of solving the sub problem presented in
the active set. This can lead to problems as the LDL′ factorization requires the factorized matrix
to be square and symmetric positive deﬁnite. Where symmetry is ensured by deﬁnition of the KKT
matrix, positive deﬁniteness is not.
11
==PAGE:38==
==PAGE:39==
Chapter 4
Algorithms for Convex Quadratic
Optimization
This chapter details the algorithms need for convex quadratic optimization and few specializations
that can be used when solving. This details the Active Set method that is used in this theses and
the solver specializations that can be applied. This chapter is based on [17, 19, 35].
4.1
Introduction
This chapter discuss the methods of solving the basic quadratic problem with inequality constraints
of the form
min
x
1
2xT Hx + gT x + γ
s.t.
a′
ix −b ≥0
i ∈I
(4.1)
Where H ∈Rn×n is a symmetric and positive semi-deﬁnite matrix. Furthermore, let x ∈Rn; λ ∈
Rm; g ∈Rn; b ∈Rm; A ∈Rn×m be deﬁned as such. Establishing the matrix H is positive deﬁnite
ensures that no stationary points which are local minima, local maxima or saddle points are present.
Equation (4.1) is the foundation for this chapter and if any extensions are included in the methods
this is explicitly stated and the full problem with any extensions will follow.
The dimensionality is very important for all parts of this thesis and special attention to the
dimension of the constraint matrix is important. i.e. A ∈Rn×m. This is the only form that A will
take. The following section will follow [35, p. 472] and [5].
A primal Active Set method for solving convex quadratic programs is typically a two phase approach,
the ﬁrst phase ﬁnds an initial feasible point and phase two locates optimality in an iterative process
while maintaining feasibility. There exits primal and dual Active Set methods each handling the
solution method slightly diﬀerent but the framework is very similar. Below the primal method is
elaborated.
4.2
Primal-Active Set
In this section the primal Active Set method for solution of a strictly convex quadratic program
with only inequalities is discussed. The basic idea of the algorithm is detailed for the problem (4.1).

The Active Set algorithm iterates though the set of constraints Wk, the working set, that is a subset
of the active set Aw = {i : i ∈I} where constraints are iteratively added or removed depending
whether the constraints are active or not, i.e. if a′
ix −bi = 0, ∀i ∈I. Throughout the iterations
the objective function is minimized and ﬁnally the optimum x∗and the corresponding optimal
active set A∗

ix∗= b} is found. If the optimal active set A∗

.

convex problem can be solved by solving the corresponding KKT system by any available method.
This master thesis will focus on the Null Space method. The Null Space method is the most
eﬀective when there are a small degree of freedom i.e. n −m is small [5]. Where x ∈Rn and
m = |Aw| is the number of constraints in the working set. Yet, naturally the optimal working set
A∗
w is not known before hand and optimal solution to the problem neither.
Due to the iterative process of the Active Set algorithm, the problem of ﬁnding the optimal working
set A∗
w in a problem with many constraints can prove to be diﬃcult and an interior point algorithm
could be preferable. This leads to the deﬁnition of the QP,

4.2.2

This section is discuss key aspects of the Linear Problem(LP) and its solution methods. It is based

To solve this system of equations the Simplex method can be used in basic form by applying
Gaussian elimination. During the iterative process the objective value is gradually improved until
optimum is found. This method is similar to the Active Set algorithm proposed for QP’s and is
also categorized as an Active Set algorithm, yet is called the Simplex method and is only applicable
for linear programs.

4.2.4
Iterative Process for the Active Set Algorithm
The problem will be initialized by a given x0 within the feasible space, x0 ∈Ωand a working set W0
that is in fact a subset of the Active Set, W0 ⊆Aw(x0), as the algorithm relies on the constraints
in the working set to be linearly independent. The active constraints in the initial feasible point
can be found by calculating A′x0 −b and the active constraints will adhere to A′x0 −b = 0.
In the generation of feasible solutions xk it is found that the relationship xk + p = xk+1, p being
step size. Finding the step size p is found to simplify the computations needed for new optimal
point xk+1 ∈Ω.
This then leads to
f(xk+1) = f(xk + p) = f(xk) + x′

for improving the objective function and the relationship follows f(xk) > f(xk+1) = f(xk + p).
The ﬁrst order terms x′

When solving the subproblem two diﬀerent situations arise where p∗= 0 or p∗̸= 0. In the second
case any xk + αp∗, α ∈[0, 1] must be feasible in the aﬃne space created by the working set Wk. It
is then seen that the line segment created by f(xk + αp∗), α ∈[0, 1] is feasible with regards to the
aﬃne space of Wk, yet not all values of α may be feasible in the complete feasible space Ω. Thus a
line search is warranted to ﬁnd the best value for α while xk + αp∗∈Ω.
Proposition 4.2.2 (Line Search). Let xk ∈Ω∩Mk where Mk is the aﬃne space created by
Mk = {x ∈Rn : a′

Ω= {x ∈R : a′

Proof. Given in [5]
Now if α∗= 1 then the largest reduction possible within the aﬃne space has been found to be
xk+1 = xk + p∗and then the current working set Wk is carried on to next iteration, Wk = Wk+1.
In this situation the next iteration will ﬁnd p∗= 0 and then the optimal solution has been found,
unless numerical diﬃculties arise.
If α∗is found to be less than one i.e. α ∈(0, 1) then xk + p∗is not feasible with respect to at least
one constraint in the polyhedron Ω. Then there exists a j ∈I \ Wk such that a′
j(xk + α∗p∗) = bj
and the corresponding constraint j is called a blocking constraint. This constraint is then added to
the working set Wk+1 = Wk ∪{j} and current xk is updated with αkpk xk+1 = xk + α∗p∗.
Finally consider when p∗̸= 0 and α∗= 0; then blocking constraints are found in the current solution
xk. Meaning that more constraints must be added to the working set Wk without improvement of
the solution. This can only happen n times, as after O(n) iterations the constraint matrix will be
of full rank and p∗will then be found to be zero.
Now consider the case when p∗= 0; in this situation xk is known to be feasible and calculations of α
are unnecessary. This situation occurs when the current working set restricts further improvement of
the objective function along the constraints but one or more constraints are blocking the possibility
of improvement. In both situation the Lagrangian multipliers are calculated to ﬁnd any possibility
of improvement. Alternatively optimal solution is found. To calculate the Lagrangian multipliers,
the λ from the KKT conditions must be found, i.e. λ in
X
i∈Wk
aiλi = Hxk + g
(4.13)

must be found. This is n equations with |Wk| ≤n unknowns. If the computed multipliers λi

λi i /∈Wk must fulﬁll the KKT conditions and thus equal to zero.

Contrarily, if there exists a j such that λj < 0 for j ∈Wk then there is a possibility of improvement
of the objective function by removing the corresponding constraint from the working set, i.e.
Wk+1 = Wk \ {j}, j ∈Wk, λj < 0 and further more a possible decrease of the objective function.
Furthermore, if multiple indexes j such that uj < 0 for j ∈Wk exists then the j to exit the working
set chosen will be j = arg min
j∈Wk{uj}.
Linear independence is required of the constraint coeﬃcients that are present in the matrix, Aw,
constituted by the initial working set W0 and all subsequent working sets Wk, k = 1, 2, .... This is
ensured when blocking constraints are added to the working set. Then the blocking constraint can
not be a linear combination of the ai, i ∈Wk in the current working set, as otherwise it would not
be blocking. Thus linear independence is preserved by additions of blocking constraints. On the
other hand, deletions of indexes from the working set, corresponding to deletions of columns in the
constant matrix Aw surely can not introduce linear dependency.
The steps of the method are now summarized into algorithm 5 seen underneath

4.2.5
Specialized Null Space Solver
The subproblem (4.9) that is solved sets the b vector to zero and thus solving R1xY = b becomes
arbitrary and xY should be set to zero. This means that the ﬁrst forward substitution of algorithm
1 can be removed and simpliﬁed into algorithm 6.
The specialization of the Null Space solver reduces the number of operations needed to 6n2 by
removing the one forward substitution i.e. R1xY = b corresponding to removing one n2 operations.
6n2 is found by one forward, backward substitution and a diagonal multiplication for the LDL′
solver resulting in 2n2 + n. x = Q2xZ is a matrix-vector multiplication operations and ﬁnally
R1λ = Q′
1(H · x + g) is 3n2 + n leading to 6n2 + 2n ≈6n2.
Algorithm 6 Null −space specialized Solver
1: QR factorize A matrix into Q = [Q1 Q2]′ and R = [R1 0]′.
2: Solve (Q′
2 · H · Q2)xZ = (−Q′
2 · g) using the LDL′ factorization of Q′
2 · H · Q2
3: Calculate solution x = Q2 · xZ;
4: Calculate dual values and apply Forward substitution R1λ = Q′
1 · (H · x + g)

Chapter 5
Linear Algebra
This section contains the majority of the theory for the basic operations needed to accomplish
many of the methods used in the complete optimization algorithms. Focusing on the higher level
matrix operations/functions/methods for factorizations and corresponding updates that is relevant
for the work done in this master thesis. While these methods are described essential considerations
for computations are also discussed and an in depth review of a Matrix-Matrix multiplication is
discussed to exemplify how computations are eﬀected by the implementation of operations.
5.1
Matrix Operations
5.1.1
Matrix Multiplication
This section shortly describes simple optimizations that can be done to the matrix operations to
speed up sub-results that are essential for the optimization algorithms. This section will be based
on the applied Matrix-Matrix multiplication.
5.1.1.1 Matrix-Matrix Multiplication.
The discussion upon Naive matrix multiplication is based on the paper [24].
Theroem 5.1.1. Given A ∈Rm×p, B ∈Rp×m and C ∈Rm×n. Then the inner product between two
matrix multiplication given by A · B = C and follows ﬁgure 5.1
The simple method of matrix multiplication, also called the naive method needs O(n3) Floating
point operations per second (ﬂops) to complete, and follows algorithm 7. This method consists of
three nested loops and is a straightforward method to implement this operation.

1: for i=1:m do

Fig. 5.1: Matrix matrix multiplication of A · B = C illustration the steps needed to ﬁnd each element of
the resulting matrix C from A and B.
Yet, this is an ineﬃcient method for the matrix calculations as it is intensively memory bound. That
is an operation that is restricted not by the processing speed of the processor but by the bandwidth
of the memory, how quickly the data can be moved from memory to processor. Algorithm 7 is
memory bound due to the method that computers store matrices. Most common is row- or column-
major which translates into the method that computer stores the matrix as an one-dimensional
array where rows or columns are stored consecutively. Furthermore each memory read does not
only read the single number that is wanted but a so called cache line and this translates into a
consecutive number of memory slots are read at each memory read. The length of the cache line is
speciﬁed from the processor vendor and is at this point in time commonly 32 or 64 bits. When
single or double precision ﬂoating point data is used then it for a 64 bit system reads 64/4 = 16 or
64/8 = 8 consecutive numbers for single or double precision.
Now assuming row-major the read operations on the A matrix are sequential, allocated coalesced
memory access, where in contrast the memory reads needed for each iteration of the B matrix is
non-sequential and thereby need many more memory reads/strides to complete. This is ineﬃcient
as the memory strides are a time consuming process and often need many more clock cycles to
complete. Worst case if the cache can not contain a full row of the matrix this will result in O(p2)
memory strides, p being the 1st dimension of B. Memory reads of the B matrix are in this case
non-coalesced and resulting in many cache misses. A cache miss is a state where the requested
memory is not in cache and therefor resulting in a memory fetch that causes delays in execution.
A solution that is eﬀective is to reorder the nested for loops such that cache misses do not occur as
frequently. These are tested and proven to be a simple and eﬀective way of getting almost 7.5 fold
speed up, reducing the time from approximately 23 seconds to 3 for a 1024 × 1024 matrix. In table
5.1 the run times of six diﬀerent orderings of the for loops are included where the letter ordering
corresponds to the ordering of the for loops there algorithm 7 is NaiveIJK. It is important to
ensure that no signiﬁcant parts of the matrix can be stored in lower level cache when tests are done.
22
==PAGE:49==
5.1.
Matrix Operations
By not ensuring this will result in identical running times for all orderings.
Further emphasizing the ineﬃciency is the fact that to complete the computation of the part result
c1,2 (marked by the red-yellow dot in ﬁg 5.1) b11, b12, b13 is also moved into registers assuming that
one row ﬁts in the registers. Every time a cell from B is needed the a cache line of architecture
based length is read, in row ordering. This means even though only one element of the memory
read is needed three values are moved and only one is used. This results in ﬁlling the cache
with unnecessary data and doing unnecessarily many memory reads. The situation is known as a
non-coalesced memory access with cache misses on each iteration. A solution to this is to transpose
the B matrix, this leads to an extra memory allocation and transfer, but since transposing is only
an O(n2) this can still improve performance.
Algorithm 8 Transposed Matrix-Matrix multiplication
1: tmp ∈Rp×n
2: for j=1:n do
3:
for k=1:p do
4:
tmp[k, i] = B[k, j]
5:
end for
6: end for
7: for i=1:m do
8:
for j=1:n do
9:
for k=1:p do
10:
C[i, j] = A[i, k] · B[k, j]
11:
end for
12:
end for
13: end for
This method is not always feasible if strict memory resections are present. To improve the matrix
calculations it would therefore be beneﬁcial to use more of the numbers loaded by each cache line
from B. This results in multiple elements of the C matrix to be calculated per iteration. This
means if it is chosen to use two scalars from each cache line of B per iteration the cache line misses
are cut in half, therefore to maximize performance using the full L1 cache1 line is optimal. This
is machine speciﬁc but for many modern computers it is now 64 bits per line. Therefore 8 or 16
scalars can be loaded per iteration depending whether float or double is used.(or single or double
precision is used.) The resulting sudo code is seen in algorithm 9.
1The L1 cache is the lowest level cache present in the processor .

17:
end for
18:
end for
19:
end for
20: end for
This algorithm is more complex but the six nested for loops present now accomplish the matrix
multiplication using the full cache line. Where the three outer loops iterate through the matrix
in intervals of SM that corresponds to the length of scalars that ﬁt in each cache line. The three
inner loops then iterate over each j2 and k2, where it is done in diﬀerent order compared to the
naive model as the resulting values only depend on one k2 but two on j2.
Finally modern processors implement hardware accelerated vectorization where the processor
possesses the possibility to process multiple values at once. There extensions are called Single
Instruction, Multiple Data (SIMD) operations. Intel implements a set of hardware instructions
called Advanced Vector Extensions (AVX) that require special sets of commands for the coding and
compiler, but seem beneﬁcial for the execution time. A second method applying AVX instructions
is applied, this method uses an AVX instruction to read columns instead of rows and is tested
under the name mmultAVX2.
This corresponds to a vectorized approach to matrix multiplication and is illustrated in 5.2 where
multiple elements of the matrix C are calculated simultaneously.
Fig. 5.2: Vectorized matrix multiplication
Simpliﬁed this is equivalent to switching the two inner most for loops in algorithm 7 but further
adjustments are necessary to fully utilize the cache of A B and C completely. Adjustments of the

5.2
Matrix Factorizations
This section describes the factorizations that are used in the master thesis. The main focus is on the
QR and LDL′ factorizations as these two methods are the main factorizations used for the proposed
implementations of the Active Set algorithm. The methods are extended with updating schemes to
asymptotically achieve a more eﬃcient iteration in the Active Set algorithm. Factorizations refer
to the decomposition of a mathematical object, in this thesis matrices are focused on. They are
often applied to reduce the number of operations needed to solve the linear system Ax = b.
5.2.1
QR Factorization
The QR factorization is based of the proposals from [14, 23] and underlying theory to complete the
QR factorizations is also presented in this section.
The QR factorization of any matrix A ∈Rn×m such that
A = QR = [Q1 Q2]

5.2.2
Givens Rotations
The section detailing Givens rotations is based on the work of [23, chapter 5] and [14, chapter 17].
The Givens rotations can be used to introduce zeros into a matrix A, and thus if applied correctly
it can compute the Q and R matrix from A. This is achieved by introducing zeros into the matrix
A such that it becomes upper triangular, resulting in the R matrix, and simultaneously multiplying
an identity matrix with the same rotations produce the Q matrix.
The Givens rotation is a plane rotation that is applied to a matrix, in our case a matrix in Rn×m,
such that the rotation is applied to the rows i and j. The rotation is applied to the matrix A with
an angle θ. This then results in the deﬁnition;
Deﬁnition 5.2.2. A matrix of the form (5.2) is a Givens matrix. The value c is on the diagonal at
(i, i) and (j, j) , where i < j. The value −s is at index (j, i), and s is at index (i, j). The remaining
diagonal entries are 1, and all remaining elements are zero.
It is required that the Givens rotation is orthogonal, i.e. G(i, j, θ)′G(i, j, θ) = I, thus it is required
that c2 + s2 = 1
G(i, j, θ) =

Therefore any angle θ for the Givens rotation can be used.
Remark 4. When applying the Givens rotations it is seen that it is only the column where the

5.2.3
Householder Reﬂection
The section detailing Householder reﬂection is based on the work of [23, chapter 5] and [14, chapter
17]. Using Householder reﬂections is an alternative method to the Givens rotations to introduce
zeros into the matrix. The Givens rotation introduces zeros one element at a time and a series of
27
==PAGE:54==
5.
Linear Algebra
rotations are needed to transform the matrix into upper triangular from that is needed for the QR
factorization. Contrary to this the Householder reﬂections zero out all the elements in the same
column under the diagonal aii in one iteration. This results in the algorithm needing signiﬁcantly
fewer iterations to achieve the upper triangular form. Yet applying the Givens rotations makes the
algorithm simpler to parallelize, as it only operates on two rows at a time, and it is necessary for
the Householder reﬂections to be applied to the full matrix simultaneously.
The Householder reﬂection is a Rn×n orthogonal matrix formed from a vector in Rn. This means
that the constructing vector must ﬁrst be found. The vector is found when understanding the
deﬁnition and properties of the reﬂection.
Deﬁnition 5.2.3. A Householder reﬂection Hv is a transformation that takes the vector v and
reﬂects it about the plane Rn
Hv = I −τvv′,
τ =
2
v′v , v ∈Rn, H ∈Rn×n
(5.5)
The Householder reﬂection also has the following properties
Theroem 5.2.4. Let Hv be the Householder reﬂection of v ∈Rn, then
a) Hv is symmetric
b) Hv is orthogonal
c) H2
v = I
d) Hvu = u if ⟨v, u⟩= 0
The target is now to ﬁnd a vector v such that the Householder reﬂection will introduce zeros.
5.2.3.1 Column Zeroing Using Householder Reﬂection.
As discussed previously the Householder reﬂection can be used to zero out a series of elements in
the same column of a given matrix A. This is applied to any position in the matrix and thereby
introducing zeros into the matrix but for our application it is only needed to introduce zeros under
the diagonal aii. Thus the objective is to transform an arbitrary matrix,

To avoid under or overﬂow errors while calculating the norm the following procedure is implemented
a) colmax = max{|x1|, |x2|, ..., |xn|}
b) ¯x = x/colmax
c) ∥x∥2 = colmax · ∥¯x∥2

Lemma 5.2.5. Let Hv be a Householder reﬂection given by v and k an arbitrary constant. Then the
Householder reﬂection of k · v is equal Hv, i.e. Hv = Hkv.

In this thesis the two algorithms are combined to achieve less computations, while still using the

current multi core architecture. Methods also implement column pivoting to handle rank deﬁciency
however this is not a problem that will be discussed further in this thesis. Some of these methods
are described by [9, 12, 39].

5.2.5
Asymptotic Analysis of The QR Factorization using Givens Rotations
Now the algorithm for ﬁnding the QR decomposition using Householder rotations is stated, for
comparison reasons the QR decomposition using Givens rotations is shown.
Algorithm 13 QR factorization using Givens rotations
1: Q = I
2: for i = 1 : min{m −1, n} do
3:
for j = i + 1 : m do
4:
[c, s] = givens(a[i, i], a[j, i])
5:
A[[i, j], i : end] = g · A[[i, j], i : end]
6:
Q[[i, j], 1 : end] = g · Q[[i, j], 1 : end]
7:
end for
8: end for
9: R = A
10: return Q = Q′
Calculation of the Givens rotation takes 8 ﬂops. Observing here that it can be done in 7 ﬂops as
the calculation of r is not necessary as it is not returned in algorithm 10, but it can have small
complexity beneﬁts if used correctly. Assuming the general case m > n then the QR decomposition
Table 5.3: Flops needed for one iteration Givens based QR factorization
Computation
Flop Count
Givens rotation
7
Updating R
6n
Updating Q
6m
takes (7 + 6(m + n)) Pn
i=1(m −i) iterations. This is then expanded into 3n(mn + 2m2 −n2) and if
n = m it boils down to 6n3, that is O(n3).
Comparing the two methods, using Givens rotations or Householder reﬂections, to compute the
QR factorization it is seen that they are asymptotically similar but applying the Givens rotations
requires approximately twice as many computations before removing the ﬁnal lower order terms.
This leads to the conclusion that the Householder based QR factorizations are the choice for
factorization while not exploiting any structure nor having prior knowledge of the matrix at hand.
Remark 6. Note the QR factorization is not unique. This is emphasized when comparing the QR
factorization using the two diﬀerent methods. When the decomposed matrix A is not of full rank
this becomes apparent as the upper right corner of the Q component will be zero and it’s size is
n −m. This is not seen to have an impact on the Null Space method when solving linear equations
but is seen when debugging the program.
5.2.5.1 Other Implementations. The Householder reﬂections present an eﬃcient method of
zeroing out columns as needed for the QR factorization yet it leads to a problematic parallel
implementation as the full matrix is needed for each iteration. This leads to an algorithm that is
not utilizing the modern hardware, based on multi core environments.

==PAGE:58==
5.
Linear Algebra
A blocked version of algorithm 11 exist which completes some of the computations asynchronously,
and thereby implementing partly parallel version as by [9].
5.3
Updating the QR Factorization
The implementation of the QR factorization is O(n3) thus it is beneﬁcial to investigate if a possible
updating scheme would be more optimal. Initially, updating the QR factorization in each step of
the Active Set algorithm is proposed. This updating scheme is then applied if theoretically faster
and are then tested.
The updates that are proposed are additions and deletions of the columns in the A matrix. This
corresponds to the addition or deletion of a constraint in the working set of the Active Set algorithm.
When these additions or deletions are carried out orthogonal eliminations, also referred to as Givens
rotations, are employed to restore the triangular structure of the matrix R. The same rotations are
also applied to Q and incorporated to update the LDL′ factorization. The details are discussed in
the following section. The methods are analyzed theoretically to evaluate if these techniques are
beneﬁcial theoretically with out any implementation speciﬁc considerations or not.
The methods detailed in this section are based on [17, 18, 30].
5.3.1
Additions to the QR Factorization
When adding a constraint from the KKT system, it is equivalent to the addition of a column in the
QR factorization of A, where the k’th column, k ∈[0, m + 1], to A. Thus A is updated to ˜A and
written;
˜A = [A(1 : n, 1 : k)] ak A(1 : n, k + 1 : m)]
and the updated QR becomes
Q′ ˜A = [R(1 : n, 1 : k −1) rk R(1 : n, k + 1 : m)] = ˜R
where ak denotes the additional column to be added to A. ak can be added at an arbitrary index k
yet adding at m + 1 is later shown to be the most eﬃcient. rk denotes the column that is added
to R as a result of the changes in A. The procedure to update R is to insert ak into R at the
appropriate location and then restore the upper triangular from of R by applying Givens rotations
to zero out non-zeros in ak. The product of all the Givens rotations used is the matrix G,G2 being
the aﬀected part of G, i.e.
G =
"
I
0
0
G2
#
(5.8)
The updated Q is then denoted ˜Q and computed by multiplying all the Givens rotations used for
R to Q, i.e,
˜Q = GQ =
"
Q1
G2Q2
#
.
(5.9)
The location of the inserted column into R has no eﬀect on the Active Set algorithm, and therefore
the optimal insertion point can be chosen. Considerations on how may computations that are
needed to fulﬁll the update are discussed.

only (m −k) Givens rotations needed to be applied. No further matrix calculations are need to the
right of insert and the fewest possible applications of Givens rations is needed. This is illustrated

algorithm completes the full QR factorization.

5.3.2
Deletions to the QR Factorization
When removing a constraint from the KKT system, it is equivalent to the deletion of a column in
the QR factorization of A, where the k’th column, k ̸= m, From A. Thus A is updated to ˜A and
written,
˜A = [A(1 : n, 1 : k −1)] A(1 : n, k + 1 : m)]
and thus the updated QR becomes
Q′ ˜A = [R(1 : n, 1 : k −1) R(1 : n, k + 1 : m)]
The deleting procedure is split into diﬀerent cases dependent on the dimensions of A ∈Rn×m and
the choice of index, k, for the deleted column. The possible range of outcome for deletions are
summed up in the following cases:
– m ≤n This is be the most likely interval during the iterations.
Further the complexity of deletions depend on deletion point k
– k < m Removing an interior constraint in the A matrix.
– k = m Removing the last constraint in A.
– m > n This outcome is not present in the Active set algorithm as the maximal amount of
constraints added to the working set is be equal to n.
The complexity of the deletion is highly depended on the location of the deleted column and is
discussed in the following section.
When a deletion of an interior constant is executed the Givens rotations are applied to restore the
upper triangular form of the R matrix. This is seen in the following illustration where ⊖represents
the locations needed to be zeroed out by the Givens rotations.
Let R ∈R6×5, ˜R ∈R6×4,
˜Rk=2 =

It is illustrated how the choice of k has a major impact on the amount of Givens rotations which
need to be applied. Further, if k = m then the least possible Givens rotations are needed but this
situation can not be guaranteed during the iterations of the Active Set algorithm.

5.3.3
Asymptotic Analysis of the Additions and Deletions to the QR
Factorization
Now that the algorithms for deletions are deﬁned a thorough analysis of the methods and their run
time is completed to determine if the implementation is beneﬁcial compared to a full factorization.
As stated the amount of Givens rotations necessary to complete the Givens rotations if highly
aﬀected by the value of k, and it is seen if k = 1 the worst case situation is achieved.
Additions in the worst case scenario results in m−1 iterations with 7 ﬂops per calculation of Givens
rotations and 2(m −1) multiplications when applying the rotation to the R matrix. This results in
O(m2) and to update the Q matrix the amount of calculations are be doubled, but still resulting in
O(n2) ﬂops. Note when calculating the updated Q matrix the operations are in terms of n as the Q
matrix is not upper triangular and therefor the Givens rotation must be applied to the full column.
In the worst case scenario deletions require m −1 iterations of the for loop and then each iteration
would apply the Givens rotation requiring 7 ﬂops and the application requiring 2(m −1) = O(m)
multiplications. Therefore the full deletion needs (m −1) · (7 + 2(m −1)) which forms into O(m2)
ﬂops.
Comparing to factorizing from basis each time the additions or deletions method is applied reduces
the computations by a power of one. It is beneﬁcial to produce the new QR factorization by
updating the existing factorization compared to reinitializing. The deletions are assumed to be the
worst case scenario, and for the general case it is seen to be more eﬃcient.

5.4.4
Asymptotic Analysis of Additions and Deletion to the LDL’
Factorization
Additions are carried out by calculating the Hz matrix and is accomplished by executing memory
swaps instead of multiplying by ˜I resulting in only 2(n−m)2 ﬂops compared to 5n3 when computing
Hz from D
1
2 L′ ˜IQ′
2 ˜I, m being the number of constraints in the working set. Reduction in equation
(5.19) using Givens rotations results in (n −m)2 multiplications and ﬁnish by multiplying into L
and D in (n −m −1)(n −m −2) + n −m −1 ≈(n −m −1)2 using the structure of both L and D.
To ﬁnd ¯Z needed during deletions the reordering of ˜Q is carried out, but as in additions it is
unnecessary to multiply ˜Q by ˜I, instead the ordering can be reversed in memory. This means
no computations are needed for this step. Forming ZHz needs n2 + (n −m)n. Now the back
substitution needs (n −m)2 [18, 20].
It is then seen that both deletions and additions the updating methods remove the cubed term of
doing the LDL′ factorization from basis. The factorization constants on the squared terms are
higher for updating could prove to de dominating when the algorithm is applied on minor problems.
5.5
Cholesky Factorization
The Cholesky factorization is similar to the LDL′ factorization except for the omission of the
diagonal. This section about the Cholesky factorization is based on [8]. This then necessitates a
square root in the calculations. Square roots is considered a time consuming operation but oﬀsets
the computational beneﬁts of this factorization compared to the LDL′ when using small matrices,
yet since the number of square roots only scales linearly as a factor of n, therefore the aﬀect will
decrease as the dimensions increase.

5.6
Asymptotic Analysis
Alalysing the factorization time the QR factorization is not the fastest, where Cholesky is more
favorable shown in table 5.4. The rationale behind using the QR factorization for the general
solving of QP’s is the possibilities of updating the factorization and further more the factorization
is not applied to the full KKT matrix as would be the case for a solver based on the Cholesky
factorization. Justifying use of the QR factorization further are key conditions necessary when
applying LU, Cholesky and LDL′ that must be met, as otherwise the factorization will fail. These
include symmetry, positive semi deﬁniteness and for non-permutating implementations a well
conditioned matrix. Not all need to be fulﬁlled for LU, Cholesky and LDL′ but at least one or
more.

Chapter 6
Specializations to Inequality Constrained QP
This chapter details the specializations that are needed to implement the updating methods that
are detailed in chapter 5. This includes additional steps for the Active Set method needed when
adding or removing constraints from the working set. Furthermore, a theoretical comparison is
made showing lower operation count for the Active Set method when applying updates to the QR
and LDL′ factorizations. These specializations follow the work of [16].
6.0.1
Primal Active Set Algorithm With Updates
Using the updating methods described in chapter 5, the the Active Set algorithm can now be
revised to include the updating of the factorization.
Algorithm 5 is modiﬁed to include the updating scheme, when deletions or additions are executed.
This results in a change after line 11 in algorithm 5 where updating methods are to be added
when constraints are removed corresponding to a column of matrix A being removed This then
computes as a deletion in QR factorization and an addition to the LDL′ factorization. Similarly
by adding the updating methods when a constraint is added to the working set is done on line 18
of the algorithm 5, this then results in an addition in QR factorization and a deletion to the LDL′
factorization. This is given in the following algorithm 22.
Due to the method used to update the LDL′ factorization, the Null space solver uses the Z matrix,
to solve the problem in the place of Q2. This has no inﬂuence on the solution but only on the
intermediate steps of the solver.
Deﬁnition 6.0.1. Let A ∈Rn×m and Q, R be the matrix QR factorized from A such that A = QR.
Furthermore let Q = [Q1 Q2] and R = [R′
1 0]′. Then Z is to be deﬁned such that Z = Q2 ˜I where
˜I is the identity matrix with ﬂipped horizontal axis. Then Z and Q2 both produce the Null Space
when post multiplied with A′ that is AZ = 0.

While implementing this it is seen that the method for adding a constraint when the working set
is empty is equivalent to applying the QR factorization using Givens rotations. In the practical
implementation the active set Aw will be factorized using the Householder reﬂections only if the
initial working set is non-empty, and otherwise the Givens rotations will be used though the QR
add update method. This does does not aﬀect the number of iterations of the Active Set method
signiﬁcantly.

is the only diﬀerence in the two proposed methods for solving the QP. This means that the two

When comparing Active Set methods with and without updates it is seen that the updating method
removes all the quadratic terms in the solving the problem. Thereby it can be concluded that the
updating scheme gives the prospect of major computational time improvements especially when
the problem size increases.
It is noted that the method for deriving the QR factorization has an impact on the Null Space
solver, that is whether the QR factorization, more speciﬁcally the Q matrix, is produced using the
Givens rotations or the Householder reﬂections. When the Givens rotations are used the resulting
Q matrix will have a zero triangle in upper right corner of the dimensions m −n −1. This becomes
an error invoking fault when the method of producing the Q matrix in the Active Set algorithm
does not reset the QR factorization and re factorize from basis when |Aw| = 0 to |Aw| = 1.
6.2
Computational Comparison Using Benchmark Problems
The problems that are used to benchmark the Active Set with and without updating methods will
be simulated test problems and this section will describe how they are generated.
6.2.1
Test problems
The test problems are generated randomly by the following method. To ensure consistency in the
test results a ﬁxed seed is set for the random generator. The positive deﬁnite Hessian matrix H is
generated by multiplying a matrix of random numbers, X ∈Rn×n, with it’s own transpose and
43
==PAGE:70==
6.
Specializations to Inequality Constrained QP
adding an identity matrix to increase the determinant. Increasing the determinant of the matrix
decreases the conditioning number κ with the added beneﬁt of increasing precision and ensuring
linear independence.
H = X′ · X + I.

is ensured of the matrix G.

Generation of the convex hull Ωthat is constructed from the constraint matrix A is also needed for
testing the algorithm. In this master thesis a dense matrix are used and therefore the A matrix can
be generated randomly. To generate the right hand side, b, of the equation system ﬁrst two vectors
x and λ will also be produced. These vectors will produce an interior point in the convex hull of
the ﬁnal test problem. Then the vectors x and λ′ will be used to produce a g vector by multiplying
by the matrices H and −A such that g = Hx −A′λ. An interim b vector will be generated by
multiplying A′ with x. The system that is generated now is equivalent to an overdetermined
equality constraint QP, as all the constraints intersect in x. To convert this into an inequality
constrained problem the interim b vector can be transformed element wise by the addition of an
arbitrary ϵ ∈R. To produce a convex hull with x as an interior point the inequality constraints
will be determined by the sgn(ϵ) added to each bi, i = 1, ..., m, i.e.
Ai(x) =




aix −bi ≥0
ϵ < 0
aix −bi = 0
ϵ = 0.
(6.1)
Though the third case ϵ = 0 should be avoided, as equality constrains should not be present in this
test process.

Chapter 7
Implementation
This section discusses the considerations needed when implementing in C and also detailing the
choices made during the programming phase of the thesis.
7.1
Introduction
It is desired to have a method that solves the key aspects of the optimization, that is eﬃciency
and eﬀectiveness of the program to ﬁnd optimum of the QP problem. These two aspects are often
seen to be contradictory in practice. Where precision is achieved by increasing the data size and
thereby degrading speed. These problems do not arise when handling the mathematical theory of
the process at hand.
While the theory of mathematical optimization shows us that the operations return the desired
results and as shown in chapter 5 to exact precision. When transferred to computer arithmetic
there is always a ﬁnite precision, caused by the translation to binaries. A simple diﬀerence in choice
of type (single or double precision) has lasting eﬀects on the performance of the program.
To achieve and complete the objective for this master thesis the results are also implemented to
show real aspects of the theory in question. This includes computational considerations on what
determines performance and precision of the methods applied. All implementations are non-parallel.
7.2
Implementation
This thesis implements all methods without any use of preexisting linear algebra libraries. Therefore,
chapter 5 thoroughly details the mathematical theory that are necessary for the complete program
to function. Within the part few discussions upon the implementations are made but some remarks
exist to highlight key issues or considerations for computations. This section details and discuss
the implications of implementation speciﬁc problems.
Most crucial is the speed and precision of the implementation. To evaluate this unit tests are
implemented and the impact on results are discussed. For each part of the program an UML
diagram (A.2) is included to detail the structure of the program.
45
==PAGE:72==
7.
Implementation
7.2.1
Choice of Programming Language
For this master thesis, C has been chosen as the preferred programming language. This is evaluated
to be the most beneﬁcial for continued implementation and development. Other programming
languages exists that simplify implementation of matrix operations such as MATLAB and Python,
yet both lack computational eﬃciency in comparison to C. Furthermore, MATLAB is a propitiatory
software and operates signiﬁcantly slower than C when implementing identical methods. Yet
for both MATLAB and Python the emphasis on easy implementation has lead to more wide
spread adoption in student environment. Both languages also poses a strength to show "proof of
concept". As MATLAB is a proven platform it is used to ensure correctness of implementations and
therefore can be used to discover errors. Additionally, implementation of C permits some platform
independence. In contrast FORTRAN is also considered a very eﬃcient programming language
when applying linear algebra, but compilers for FORTRAN do not exist readily available for other
platforms than Windows, Mac OSX or Linux, and even for the Windows platform to compile a
FORTRAN program eﬃciently proprietary compilers are necessary which can be costly.
When implementing C it was chosen that the input methods should conform to industry standard
and therefore the input matrix A is of column major. As the Hessian H is symmetric this structure
is intrinsically ensured. If a more detailed study of the code is needed, then it is to be noted
that row major is used for all internal operations. This has little eﬀect on the performance of the
program, but in hindsight makes it more diﬃcult to exchange sections with other equivalent tools.
7.2.2
Data Type
The discussion of the data types is inspired by the preceding by [41, 42, 44].
The implementation of the program is based on two diﬀerent data types that both serve the same
purpose but have beneﬁts and ﬂaws. The chosen data types to be compared are single and double
precision ﬂoating points which in c99 correspond to 4 or 8 bits of memory allocation. These are
discussed in diﬀerent settings and includes sections and discuss the diﬀerent aspects on how the
choice of data type aﬀects the optimization pattern. Single precision gives approximately 8 decimals
of precision and double results in 16 decimal points. This means that any round oﬀhappen at 8
or 16 decimal point respectively, and an error of 10−8 or 10−16 is expected per operation.
The most obvious diﬀerence between these two data types is the size of the data being processed.
By changing of datatype this increases memory intensity of get and set operations for all variables.
The most compelling evidence of diﬀerence is often the memory bound operation of matrix-
matrix multiplication. Furthermore, this operation is also prone to round oﬀerrors caused by the
combination of many calculations aggregating the error and the amount of operations carried out
during execution. From chapter 5, given both matrix A and B are of dimensions n × n, O(n3) are
needed to complete this task, meaning the errors will grow in polynomial degree to the dimensions
of the matrices A and B.
The error rate for any arbitrary matrix- matrix multiplication is aﬀected by the machine precision
invoked by single and double precision stated by IEEE 752[1] is stated to 2−22 and 2−52 equivalent
to the number of digits in the mantissa. This results in 7 and 16 signiﬁcant digits and thus errors
are at most (1 + 10−8) · n3 or (1 + 10−16) · n3. This means for n = 10 the worst case error, measured
as the norm of the exact result subtracted the computed result, becomes 10−4 for ﬂoats and 10−12
but for n = 1000 this grows to an error of 1% for ﬂoats and 10−10. This indicates that the error for
one singe matrix-matrix operation is clearly aﬀected in accuracy by choice of data type. This trend
46
==PAGE:73==
7.2.
Implementation
is exponential in nature due to the computational intensity. Also, this is a signiﬁcant operation in
the Active Set algorithm, and all underlying computations and therefore any error will be further
accumulated during progression of the program, this is know as "Matrix Creep", the accumulation
of errors during multiple computations. This indicates that smaller and larger numbers can be
represented within the ﬂoat and double standard but precision is lost when more than 8 or 16
consecutive digits are represented in ﬂoat or double. An example of this in ﬂoat is the computer
equivalence of (1 + 10−8) = 1. Figure 7.1 shows the growth rate of the round oﬀerrors when
choosing between single or double precision data types. This is based on the worst case assumptions
that every element in the data has a round oﬀerror.

As stated, using doubles increases the memory usage of the operations by a factor of two as the
memory size in doubled. To investigate the implications of this increase, the optimized naive
matrix-matrix multiplication is tested again, but now only with the type diﬀerences, otherwise the
execution is identical. Here it is seen that the diﬀerence, under the proposed test are inconsequential,
and surprisingly the float operations are slightly slower. This is caused by the x86 hardware
implementation of arithmetic. The x86 is the most mainstream processor used for many computers
(Servers and personal computers) but is not often shared with smaller mobile such as phones,
tablets or others alike. This is found documented in the in instruction set of the processor, i.e.
ﬂoating point multiplication is handled by the same instruction FMUL [27].
Further testing of the aﬀect of the precision is done by comparing the results to a solution found
using MATLAB’s quadprog. The results of this this are shown in the ﬁgure 7.2 below.
From the ﬁgure 7.2 is seen that the errors are increasing for both precision choices. It is though
clear that they incur at diﬀerent speeds and for ﬂoating point operations this already becomes
unreasonable large(≥1% on average) after the problem size grows above n = 50, m = 100.
7.2.3
Ill Conditioning of the Problem
Ill conditioned problems are seen when small changes in the solution x causes large deviations
in the objective value or imposed constraints. This is seen when a matrix A ∈Rn×m is singular
or contains determinants which are approximately zero. In a situation where the problem is ill
conditioned the precision of the data is paramount as the loss of precision will greatly aﬀect the
conditioning of the problem. In the example given above (ﬁgure 7.2) the round oﬀerrors when
using float will increase likelihood of ill conditioning.

This has the conditioning of approximately κ(A) = 40002.00008. The impact of this ill conditioning
is seen when solving Ax = b, b = [1 1]′, leading to the solution achieved to be x = [1 0]′. Yet with a
small perturbation of b such that ˜b = [1.0001 0.9999]′ the solution now becomes ˜x = [3.0001 −2]′.

thus resulting in a relative error of 28285. The conditioning of the matrix is not directly related
to the chosen precision of the data but there is an indirect link between them. The numerical
instability of the matrix can be invoked by the round oﬀerrors caused by the precision.
Solution methods to the ill conditioning is described by [41, 42], and can also be achieved by
preconditioning. Both articles, by same author, investigates how increased precision help removing
problems invoked by ill conditioning and further steps to amend the problem.
To minimize errors caused by roundoﬀs in this thesis the use of double precision as the prevailing
data type is chosen. This it is also important to investigate the eﬀect of choosing doubles on
diﬀerent platforms. However, double will ensure the highest stability of the system and therefore
if no other testing is done, double should be chosen for the generic solution.
7.3
Coding Speciﬁc Considerations
When implementing the mathematical methods described in chapter 5 many considerations needed
to optimize and to use more beneﬁcial methods to increase eﬃciency are evaluated. An in depth
investigation of sequential optimization of matrix-matrix multiplication is discussed in chapter 5 as
well, to give an understanding of some of the aspects of computational optimization. The results
are implemented in the restructuring of for loops, to ensure hardware independent implementation.
Also, during the more detailed description of theory, few key points are made to give insight in
further optimization, most of which are also beneﬁcial in plain mathematical environment.
The full program implementation is assumed to be in a sequential execution platform, and thereby
few if any considerations are made for a parallel implementation. It is beneﬁcial to implement
48
==PAGE:75==
7.4.
Conclusion
methods that utilize parallel execution of this program and it is crucial to incorporate this if the
program should be more competitive.
Furthermore, it is found during implementation of the algorithms that many additional memory
operations are needed to complete a successful Active Set iteration. The active constraint matrix
Aw to be QR factorized needs to be reconstructed as well. Further matrix-matrix multiplications
are also removed as the updating method the LDL′ factorization of Z′HZ negates the need for
calculation of Z′HZ, yet the updating methods add the need for reversing the ordering of Q2 to
ﬁnd Z.
It is seen that the row major has an eﬀect on the additions and deletions when updating the QR
factorization but similar amount of memory operations are needed to rebuild the L matrix over
both storage methods. Equivalently beneﬁts can be found when creating the active constraint
matrix Aw but as the location of deletion can not be ensured this is a negligible diﬀerence.
7.4
Conclusion
During the implementation and testing of the theory, considerations such as data type choices and
the implications of choosing one over the other. It is seen choosing double precision ﬂoat as the
prevailing data type ensures signiﬁcantly smaller errors and accumulation of errors are also slower.
This then in turn ensures that errors are minimized during all operations of the Active Set method.
Additionally, problem speciﬁc situations such as ill conditioning also beneﬁt from larger precision.

Chapter 8
Tests
This chapter details the tests that are undertaken to ensure the stability and correctness of the
methods. Correctness is ensured by comparing the results to MATLAB implementations and
expecting the errors to be close to machine precision.1 These tests will test the methods that are
described in chapter 5.
8.1
Factorization Tests
The following methods will be tested in this section:
– QR factorization using Householder reﬂections
– LDL′ factorization
– Adding a column to QR factorization
– Deleting a column to QR factorization
– Adding a column to LDL factorization of Z′HZ
– Deleting a column to LDL factorization of Z′HZ
To test the factorization, it is beneﬁcial to test against an existing library for the corresponding
factorization and test the components separately to ensure less errors occurring from other methods
than the one that is tested. However, it is seen that many of the existing libraries do not explicitly
formulate the full factorization but often either compact them or return it in an economic format.
An example of this is the QR factorization by LAPACK package that returns the R matrix as the
upper triangular part of input matrix, A. Q is not returned but the vectors from the Householder
reﬂections are stored in the lower diagonal of output. This calls for a thorough testing process. It
is found that a reconstruction of the A matrix is suﬃcient for testing. This method of testing does
involve signiﬁcant computations that can lead to rounding errors, and this is taken into account
when concluding on the results. To adhere to some of the errors that can aggregate during increase
of size a relative error measurement is proposed that it takes the size of matrix into account by
comparing the 2-norm of error to the 2-norm of the matrix of which the error is measured on. That
is, for the QR factorization the error in calculation R in current implementation, compared to
MATLABs RMatLab divided by the 2-norm of RMatLab i.e.
∥R −RMatLab∥2
∥RMatLab∥2
1That is 10−16 for the chosen double precision ﬂoats.

8.
Tests
In all the tests the seed for the random generator will be set and reported such that results can
be reconstructed. The seed set for all tests is 10. While running tests a reported progress will be
given in the format In progress XXX.XX/100 where XXX.XX represents the percentage of total
progress.
All tests are run on a 2.4GHz quad core Intel cpu i5-8297U, 8 gb ram, Intel Iris Plus 655 1536 MB
Graphics and 256 gb hard disk. Full speciﬁcation sheet seen in appendix A.1.
8.1.1
QR Factorization using Householder Reﬂections
The test procedure for the QR factorization that is proposed, reconstructs A from the factors
Q and R and comparing this to the original A. To ensure that no unexpected implementation
errors which hide the errors in factorization, samples will be taken with diﬀerent matrix sizes and
compared to the MATLAB factorization. To ensure that the testing also shows stability of the
methods it will be carried out with diﬀerent dimensions matrices.
Table 8.1 shows the error generated by the factorization while comparing to MATLAB with
diﬀerent dimensions of the matrix A. These tests have been generated by QRMatLabTest.c and
the command make QRMatLabTest n=<n> m=<m>, setting <n> and <m> to the desired
sizes for the comparison with MATLAB. The test then generates the A matrix and computes
the factorization. These are then printed to a MATLAB ﬁle (QRMatLabTest.m) such that the
comparison can be made.
The relative error calculated as the 2-norm of the diﬀerence between factorization from MATLABs
implementation (RMatLab) subtracted the implementation made in C (RC) compared to the norm
of each factor. It is found that the norm of Q is one and therefore the measurement of relative
error does not add any additional information about the degree of error.

and minimum dimensions of n. All combinations of m ≤n are then tested and rerun multiple
times. The results are then collected into a .csv ﬁle named QRtest.csv.
The errors reported illustrate that the error grows when the size of the matrix grows and the
buildup of errors are correlated to the increase of both m and n dimensions.

similar movements between memory layers. This trend is only visible in non-linear plot.

8.1.3
Adding a Column to QR Factorization
Now that the basis factorizations of any arbitrary matrix are tested and shown to have high
precision, and run times are reported testing of the updating methods can commence. To run the
tests on the updating techniques of the QR factorization the same methods is used. Initially, the
methods are tested against the equivalent method in MATLAB to ensure the implementation is
correct and then many testes on arbitrary matrices is run to ensure that the updating method are
stable and eﬃcient for any matrix.
To complete the tests of QR factorization the test must be initialized by a QR factorization. Then
for this section the addition of a column will be tested. As additions of the column only will be
present when given matrix A ∈Rn×m, m < n and appending the column such that insert location
is k = m+1 and the updated ˜A ∈Rn×m+1, this is the only QR addition method that will be tested.
To run this test make testQRMatLabAdd n=<n> m=<m> is typed into the console. <n> and <m>
are the desired dimensions of the test, and a MATLAB ﬁle named QRUpdateAddMatLabTest.m is
outputted in same folder.

testing, i.e. that the tests initialize the factorization by the implemented QR factorization initializes
the tests with errors. Additional tests would be required to test this theory but as the errors are
found to be extremely small this was deemed unnecessary and it is concluded that the updating
method does not add signiﬁcant errors to the factorization.

8.1.4
Deleting a Column to QR Factorization
To test the deletions of a column the deletion location k is set randomly between 0 and m. When the
deletion location is the ending column no computations are carried out and only the reorganization
of the matrix R is needed to ensure its correctly stored in memory. This means that this operation
is almost instant and does not add errors to the factorization. The method is initially be tested
against the MATLAB implementation and there after tests will be carried out by testing the
reconstruction of the factorized matrix A. The tests against the MATLAB implementation is
reported in table 8.4. To run this test make testQRMatLabDel n=<n> m=<m> remidx=<k> is
typed into the console. <n> and <m> are the desired dimensions of the test, and <k> is the
60
==PAGE:87==
8.1.
Factorization Tests
location to remove a column, and a MATLAB ﬁle named QRUpdateDelMatLabTest.m is outputted
in the same folder.
From table 8.4 it is observed that the errors are of the same magnitude that was seen for the QR
factorization when factorizing from basis. Further, it is seen that the updating methods achieve
zeroing of the elements introduced in lower diagonal better than the basis factorization. This
means that a lowering of errors occurring by not achieving a complete zeroing can arise. As the
implementation is show to be correct to machine precision a continued test is proposed to record
more general errors and also running time for the implementation such this is compared with theory
and the complete factorization.

A more complete test of the QR factorization column delete method with k randomly set is now
made. To cater for this the environment that the Active Set method will evoke, k is randomly
chosen as the Active Set method does not ensure a speciﬁc location for the deletion. This method of
setting k is chosen primary due to two factors, ﬁrstly as just detailed it reﬂects the usage situation
and further k would be tested extremely many times if all combinations of n, m and k needed to be
carried out, without expecting a gain of insight. To decease the variance of the tests, increasing the
amount of retests are proposed but this will be restrained from as setting the maximum dimension
to be tested for to n = 150 and retesting 10 times will lead to 113250 tests. The tests are run by
typing make testQRm retests=10 maxn=150 minn=1, note the test time is very long to complete
the test with these parameters it takes approximately 10 hours on the machine used.
While evaluating the error data it were seen that there was errors that were randomly occurring.
The sample of the errors seen in table 8.5 show no speciﬁc pattern in the data. In total 162 of
these errors that misrepresented the full data set of more than 320000 data points2 after all tests
where run. These 162 data points are of a magnitude of more than 100000 as large to the rest of
the errors. This span of errors then resulted in the plot only showing the diﬀerence between these
larger errors and the rest of the points. Log plotting was also tried to adjust for the magnitude
diﬀerences but was still not suﬃcient. Additionally as the presence of these errors are so minuscule
in quantity compared to the amount of total tests run they have been deemed at outliers. The log
and non log plot of the situation described has been added to the appendix C.1. A sample of the
errors are shown in ﬁgure 8.5. This table shows no apparent pattern in the occurrence of the errors.
The error is only present when the dimensions increase beyond n = 30.
It is seen that the aggregation of errors unfold as expected; the larger the matrix becomes the
larger the degree of error. It is expected that a large part of this error is a remnant from the initial

8.1.5
Adding a Column to LDL Factorization of Z′HZ
Now that the QR updating methods are found to behaving as expected the LDL′ updating methods
are tested, as the QR updating methods are essential for the LDL updating methods. The LDL′
updating methods rely on the Givens rotations generated by the addition method. Moreover the
LDL′ factorization is carried out in on the matrix Z′HZ where Z = Q2 ˜I. Recall ˜I is the identity
matrix in reverse ordering and therefore Q2 ˜I is equivalent to ﬂipping the horizontal ordering of the
matrix Q2.
