that the results the preliminary tests are found to be suﬃciently precise the more general test of
errors are carried out. These tests are carried out as an extension of the tests proposed for updating
the QR factorization. Therefore, results in the test method for LDL add to be an extension of
the previous QR add. The test is made such that the QR factorization can still be tested solely.
Additionally, as the computation of the full QR factorization is the largest contribution time wise,
observed when comparing the run times of the diﬀerent processes needed the QR factorization ,
the test will also include column remove. The recording of results is done in two diﬀerent ﬁles,
and as both methods need the initial QR factorization procedure. Results of these are added to
previous executed tests to ensure precision of all parts of the completed tests. The results form
LDL′ add method is reported in ﬁgure 8.17. Note the non log plot is not reported here as it shows
no additional information, the ﬁgure has been put in appendix under C.2.
64
==PAGE:91==
8.1.
Factorization Tests
The test procedure follows the same methods applied previously, that is the initial factorization is
carried out for both QR and LDL′ that follow the steps in the Active Set algorithm.
a) The constraints corresponding to the active set are QR factorized, and the factors L and
D of Z′HZ matrix is initialized by the LDL′ implementation and generation of the semi
deﬁnite matrix H.
b) Then the column addition is randomly generated and the QR addition method is executed to
update the Q and R factors.
c) Finally the LDL′ updating method is applied, and the matrix Z′HZ is reconstructed from
the updated L and D factors.
This reconstruction is then compared to the same matrix Z′HZ but this is constructed by the
updated Z = ˜IQ2 returned from the QR update. The error is recorded as the 2-norm (∥· ∥2). As
this method needs exactly the same preprocessing (initialization of the QR and LDL′ factorizations)
the two methods are combined to increase eﬃciency of the tests. While the eﬃciency of the tests
are increased, the possibility of errors propagating thorough the the initial column add methods
are considered. Yet it is deemed that this method of testing mimics actual environment that the
methods are applied in and therefore if errors occur these reﬂect realistic situations and should be
handled accordingly. To run this test make testLDLUpdate retests=<nretests> maxn=<maxn>
minn=<minn> is typed into the console <nretests> corresponds to the parameter for number of
retests in the same dimension wanted. <maxn> the largest dimension to be tested on and <minn>
is the smallest, leading to intervals of dimensions to be run. Note that the number of tests increase
exponentially with n that leads to the ﬁrst tests (minn =1 maxn=50) to be completed almost
instantly and the ﬁnal (minn =140 maxn=150) taking multiple hours.
Figure 8.17 shows that the aggregation of errors occurring with the increase of the dimension n
and the decease of m. This is the same trend seen in the QR add method. This trend is caused
by errors from the initial QR and LDL′ factorization. Moreover the errors occurring from the
QR update method are propagated though the Q2 factor and the size of Q2 also increases as m
decreases so errors occurring by the increased size of the LDL′ factorization also incur. To identify
the exact location and magnitude of origin for the error it requires further investigation but as the
errors are found to be insigniﬁcantly small and thus is not deemed to be beneﬁcial nor necessary.
As before the run times for the tests have been recorded. These are reported in the two ﬁgures
8.18 and 8.19. Both plots show the same trend, that the execution time increases with the size n
and decreases when m also is increased. This is caused be the decreasing amount of computations
needed when m increases. There is a signiﬁcant increase in the interval n ∈[0, 150] in the time it
takes to complete the execution. Starting by being almost instant (10−7) but spans to 0.01 sec
when n = 150 and m set low, around 5. In such a situation it is more beneﬁcial to recompute the
LDL′ factorization, yet this will also require the reconstruction of Z′HZ matrix. Adjustments
are possible by implementing a hybrid method to increase the performance. The hybrid method
uses full factorizations when this is seen to be beneﬁcial but updates when not. However it is seen
that the speed to complete the updates when both m and n is high is also very fast and remains
high over the full interval of the problem size n. Therefore, it has not been added in the current
implementation.

column is carried out. This method is tested using the same proposed methods as seen previously.

LDL′ factorization, however the location k will still be recorded if any unexpected implications
should occur. Initially the precision of the implementation will be tested against the MATLAB
LDL′implementation and when this is found to be suﬃciently precise a more general test will
commence to test several random instance. This will ensure stability and that no unknown errors
occur.

Fig. 8.24: Log errors for LDL and QR factorization. Dimensions are listed within the graph for all plots.
8.2.1
Errors of the QR Factorization Compared to the LDL′ Factorization
When comparing the errors occurring in the QR factorization to the LDL′ factorization it is seen
the factorizations of same dimensions (blue line and red) the LDL′ poses a better precision than
the QR until approximately n = 128, where the two lines intersect, as seen in ﬁgure 8.24. Yet recall
that the dimensions of the LDL′ factorization carried out in the Active Set method is negatively
correlated to the size of m in A ∈Rn×m such that the actual errors incurring during the Active Set
method from the QR and LDL′ factorizations will be of opposite direction. That is as columns are
added to the constraint matrix A, the errors form QR factorizations will increase, but the errors
from LDL′ will decrease as the Z′HZ will decease in size.
Plotting aggregated errors then corresponds to the ﬁgure 8.25. That is ﬁgure 8.25 shows the errors
occurring from QR and LDL′ factorizations combined such that the dimensions of factors are
equivalent to the errors seen during the Active Set method.
Figure 8.25 shows that the trend of errors evoked solely from the factorizations when QR and
LDL′ factorizations are done in conjunction as expected during an iteration of the Active Set
method. As more constraints are added to the Active Set, equivalent to adding columns to the QR
factorization and removing row/column from the LDL′ then the error decreases until n > 60 for
any n and then stabilizes in the interval. This is slightly surprising result of the two factorizations
but can be attributed to the lower errors in LDL′ factorization in the lower end of dimensions
n < 60 compared to the QR factorization and then gaining a larger contribution when n > 60.

8.5
Run Time Results on Active Set Methods
While running the testes of the generated QP problem it is clear that the running times of the
updating and non-updating Active Set methods are quite diﬀerent. This is as expected when
comparing the asymptotic results from the analytical comparison. Furthermore it is seen during
implementation of the basic Active Set method where factorization reinitialized each iteration that
many additional steps are necessary to rebuild the constraint matrix and compute the left hand side
(Q′
2HQ2) needed for the Null space solver. Both are redundant when updates are implemented.
It is seen during the iterative process that the growth in the problem size in terms of the dimensions
m and n, have two eﬀects on the computation time needed, the number of iterations and the time

8.6
Conclusion on Tests
All tests have shown small error rates and run times that are as expected.
For all the tests it is shown that they compute the factorizations with very small errors and the
errors are comparable to results found in MATLAB. It is also seen that the factorizations are
stable and that they work for general dense matrices and therefore will also work for sparse, even
though they will not utilize the sparsity. While the errors did increase when the dimensions where
increased the relative error remained stable.
In a few instances an unexpected error occurs during the testing even though these errors are
signiﬁcantly larger than any other errors they are still quite small, i.e. 10−4. This requires some
further debugging to document, or mitigate this error. For all the other methods a similar behavior
was not observed.
77
==PAGE:104==
8.
Tests
All the updating methods also show the desired speed increase for each implementation. From this
it is also expected that there will be signiﬁcant speed ups of the full implementation when using
the updating methods compared to the full factorization in each step.
The updating Active Set method is seen to be advantageous compared to the basic method in
running time while retaining the same precision as basic Active Set method. The updating Active
Set method is also seen have an increased eﬃciency when the working set increases, resulting in
faster computations when more constraints are active.
78
==PAGE:105==
Chapter 9
Discussion
The results of the updating of factorizations for Active Set method show to be both more eﬃcient
and eﬀective than the basic Active set method while computing the solution to an equality
constrained problem and the proposed methods are implemented in a working program that is
stable. Furthermore, diﬀerent data types are evaluated to reﬂect on the implementation choices
and considerations are presented to justify the choices made.
A signiﬁcant improvement of the running times for the Active Set method are found when the
implementation of the updating methods were applied and tested against the Active Set methods
without the updating methods. This is in accordance with the expected result and therefore the
tests and results meet the desired goal.
While the results are as expected there are elements that need to be discussed before the ﬁnal
conclusions are drawn.
9.1
Results from Active Set Method
The tests in chapter 8 shows the running times for the updating methods were signiﬁcantly shorter
than reinitializing the factorization. This is also what the theoretical analysis presents, yet as
the methods applied in both methods are not fully optimized further speed increases of both, the
Active Set without updates and with updates are expected.
A source of errors during iterations of the Active Set is the continued accumulation of errors
originating from the calculations done during each updating procedure. No reset of the factorization
is done to ensure or rather nullify any numerical errors that are accumulated over many iterations.
To solve any accumulated errors it is assumed to be beneﬁcial to periodically reinitialize the
factorizations. It is advantageous to redo the factorization once these errors become signiﬁcant, but
the results from the two diﬀerent implementations of the Active Set method are almost identical,
seen in table 8.10, therefore it is not necessary in the current testing environment.
9.2
Code Optimization
While implementing the methods needed for solving the QP, the eﬃciency of the implementation
for each operation are not the main objective. The focus is targeted towards writing a working
implementation of both the basic and updating methods. This leaves possible optimizations to be
79
==PAGE:106==
9.
Discussion
done in the implementation. That is;
– reordering the code such that less memory operations are needed,
– ensuring no unnecessary calculations are done,
– using structure of matrices when completing matrix operations,
and many more can be optimized to decrease running time.
This is purely considering the
implementation of the code to execute faster. In chapter 5 the matrix matrix multiplication was
used as an example of a few optimizations that can be done to improve running time.
All methods that are used for both Active Set methods are implemented using sequential method
without in depth optimizations and major improvements can be made by optimizing all parts of
the code. Signiﬁcant parts of the code that is implemented for the non-updating method, are not
optimized. An example of this is the matrix-matrix products where more optimal methods are
shown but a simpler method is chosen to ensure that the implementation is not hardware speciﬁc.
It is seen that choosing an AVX implementation of the matrix matrix multiplication will improve
the speed of operations with about 1
3 yet this is an operation that is found in both implementations
of the Active Set, but more frequently in the non-updating method. This can bridge the gap
slightly while optimizing both implementations further. Additional optimizations can be achieved
by optimizing memory allocation and cache behavior but is outside the scope of the thesis, yet
would be beneﬁcial and should be investigated to increase performance further.
The main scope of the thesis is to test the performance increase by implementing updating methods
compared to reinitialization of the factorizations and this is achieved even without the fully
optimized code. This is fulﬁlled by the run times being signiﬁcantly diﬀerent and the theory also
expresses the diﬀerence should be of a full exponential degree i.e. from O(n3) to O(n2).
9.3
Datatype
It was seen in section 7.2.2 that when diﬀerent data types were chosen in the implementation
of the Active Set method that there was almost no diﬀerence in both run time and errors when
the problems were of small size, i.e. less than 25 variables and constraints. However when the
amount of variables increased further the errors occurring from round oﬀs in operations began to
be visible and when n = 100 these errors while using single precision where are signiﬁcant and the
solutions in many cases are unusable. For the system that these problems were run on there was
insigniﬁcant diﬀerence in run time between single or double precision to justify the usage of lower
precision. This identical run time performance is not expected for all platforms and therefore in
other environments the run time analysis when using diﬀerent data types could be beneﬁcial to
ensure the required speciﬁcations. In conjunction with this also testing more data types could yield
faster or more precision. This could include increasing precision to double extended being 80 bits
instead of 64 or testing implications of choosing half precision even though this would result in
unacceptable errors quickly.
9.4
Parallel and Distributed Systems
The implications of the data type will also be visible if more distributed computing systems are
used, that is if parallel and distributed computing is implemented. Some systems like GPU’s are
80
==PAGE:107==
9.5.
Phase One Problem
heavily inﬂuenced by the data type and single to double precision leads to a 50% reduction in
peak performance[36]. This is not tested in this thesis at all and in general creating a parallel
implementation of the methods that are discussed in this thesis gives an opportunity to increase
eﬃciency when the problem size grows extensively.
9.5
Phase One Problem
To complete the full QP solver the initialization of the feasible point, phase one, is also implemented
using the Revised Simplex method. This is implemented as it is a crucial part of the full solver
to initiate the primal Active Set method. The Revised Simplex method is not tested against any
other methods nor is the phase one problem compared to other methods to ﬁnd the initial starting
point. The phase one problem has not been included as part of the running time analysis for
neither theoretical nor computational results as the same method is used for both. This was chosen
as the main focus as the Active Set method for QP but by [35], the phase one problem can be a
signiﬁcant part of the execution time needed to solve the primal Active Set when no feasible point
is known beforehand. [35] states that up to 1
3 of the time can be spent on solving the phase one.
To complete the full optimization of the implementation testing and comparison of this signiﬁcant
part is also necessary.
9.6
Benchmark
The testing procedure for this program is to generate the QP problems randomly. This generation
of problems follows the methods described in section 6.2.1. This method of generating the problems
has been chosen such that the QP problems have the zero vector ˜x = 0 as an feasible point as ϵ > 0
and thus the the initial feasible point can be set to the zero vector. This implicates further that
few, if any, constrains are active in the initial point. When no constraints are active in the starting
point this means that the Active Set algorithm transverses the feasible space and primary adds
to the constraint matrix resulting in mainly executing the column add updating methods. The
proposed method is not seen to be doubtful as both updating methods (add and delete) are still
carried out. Additionally problem formulations where the zero vector is a feasible solution is also
prominent but slightly negates the need for a phase one problem solver.
This implementation is not compared to other implementations that solves the QP problem. This
would have been achieved by using standardized benchmark problems to compare results in run
time and precision of the implementation. This will also resolve the drawback of the proposed
test method for the Active Set method. Benchmarks of this sort are collected into [32] that is
a collection of QP problems and are of the ﬁle format .sif. The reader CUTEr program is then
proposed to decode these ﬁles but still a considerable amount of data decoding is needed to interface
the current implementation to the format output by CUTEr. This interfacing is beneﬁcial for
broader usage of the program and is a necessity if the program is used for more general applications
than the testing applied within this project.
An extension to the testing methods that are implemented would also be test linear algebra methods
against working implementations such as LAPACK. This would achieve two beneﬁcial tests. The
error rates of the methods would be tested against current methods to ensure stability of the
implemented method and that it fulﬁlls market standard, and also the eﬃciency could be tested to
ensure the implementation is eﬃcient and comparable to existing methods. This was not done to
the prevailing row major methods used in the current implementation, thus complicating the swap
81
==PAGE:108==
9.
Discussion
of operations and often the desired libraries did not accomplish the exact same operations that
were needed. The QR factorization is an example of this where the LAPACK method xGEQRF
returns only R explicitly and the Householder vectors, where both factors Q and R are needed.
9.7
Programming Language
The programming language C is a procedural language that is compiled to provide a low-level
memory access and to ensure programs runs fast and eﬃciently, when implemented correctly. C
is built with portability in mind thus, it can run on several computer platforms, exactly meeting
the goal for this project. The main reasons behind choosing C are the portability and eﬃciency
gains obtained comparably to other programming languages. Though these beneﬁts come at a cost
in terms of increased development time and added complexity in coding. These are no run-time
checking and lacking data security that can lead to overwriting information in the memory. When
pointers are updated with the incorrect data or index this results in incorrect memory overwrite or
worst case memory corruption. Further, most memory allocation in C forces the programmer to do
most of it themselves. C does not naively support multi threaded or distributed computing but
library have been added to accommodate this but further complicates development.
Programming languages like MATLAB and Python both handle memory allocation for the user
and have extensive run time checking to ensure correctness of passed variables and array sizes.
This results in easier implementation and error checking, and further ensures no memory errors.
Additionally, both languages utilize faster methods through methods implemented in languages like
C to complete numerical operations and thus often have the beneﬁts of being easily readable and
fast to execute the mathematical operations. This requires the operations to be implemented in C
where only parts of specialized methods can be expected implemented. This is seen in MATLAB
where the QR updating methods are present but not the LDL′ updates, that are tailored to the
speciﬁc operations in Null space solver. Additionally, implementing all operations in MATLAB or
Python often leads to slow execution. Further, the portability of MATLAB code is minimal and
it is a proprietary software that requires licensing. The conclusion is that MATLAB and Python
are great tools to apply with preexisting libraries that are eﬃciently implemented and great tools
to prototype programs, but when fast methods are needed the additional time needed to develop
programs in C or other eﬃcient language is necessary.
82
==PAGE:109==
Chapter 10
Conclusions
The main objective for the thesis is to produce a fully functioning QP solver was achieved. This
is achieved by using no dependency of any libraries or externally produced implementations.
This includes extensive implementations of methods for completing matrix operations, complete
factorizations and tieing these methods together to produce the Active Set method. All methods
are tested extensively to ensure that all produce low error rates, thereby allowing for stability of
the implementation. The basic method is then extended to include updating methods such that
the factorizations do not need to be recomputed at each iteration.
It is shown that the Active Set method can be used to solve a quadratic linear constrained problem.
To solve the problem all underlying theory is discussed and thoroughly implemented and tested to
show stability and precision of the methods proposed.
The basic Active Set method is extended to include updating methods with the goal of expediting
the iterative process of the method and this is tested to ensure stability. These methods include
updating methods for adding and deleting columns in the QR factorization, and then updating
the LDL′ factorization of the Z′HZ matrix used in the Null Space solver. The methods are
incorporated into the Active Set method and shown to improve solution time while retaining
accuracy.
All methods are analyzed showing the theoretical decrease in operations compared to completing a
full factorization. On the basis of theoretical comparison the implementation of methods is carried
out and tested. The tests show that the errors are close to machine precision and improvement in
running time against the complete factorizations. The methods are merged into the Active Set
method with few adjustments and this is tested to ensure solutions continued to be correct.
It is shown that specializing the Null Space method when it is applied to the sub problem in the
Active Set method is beneﬁcial for computational time as this directly reduces the amount of
computations needed. This specialized method was then implemented, and further developed when
applied to the updating Active Set algorithm.
The updating methods include the complete QR and LDL′ factorizations and extensions to
update these factorizations. It is seen that the two diﬀerent factorization methods show signiﬁcant
diﬀerences in execution time but also diﬀerent complexities in implementation and precision in
ﬁnal results.
It is shown that while implementing methods in a lower level language as C this results in many
extra considerations such as memory allocation and eﬃcient methods that are necessary to ensure
both eﬃciency and eﬀectiveness compared to Python or MATLAB. A few key factors such as
83
==PAGE:110==
10.
Conclusions
the data type is explored and double precision was found to be most beneﬁcial for the given
circumstance, whereas single precision is found to introduce round oﬀerrors such that problems of
size larger than 100 variables would result in errors of at least 1% magnitude.
Overall it has been successfully accomplished to produce a fully functioning QP.
84
==PAGE:111==
Chapter 11
Suggestions for Future Work
In this chapter suggestions for future work based is presented on the work conferred in this thesis.
11.1
Preconditioning and Prepossessing
Preconditioning and prepossessing of a problem is considered to be a beneﬁcial step in the
optimization of general problems as this will remove linear depended constraints. This then reduces
the size of the problem and leads to faster execution time. Preconditioning can also help reduce the
ill conditioning of problems and reduce the errors that follow from ill conditioning. None of these
methods are implemented in this solver and should therefor be considered. These beneﬁts are seen
in [7, 15] where preconditioning is applied to both accomplish speedups and better conditioning of
the problem for quadratic problems.
11.2
Additional Specializations to the Solver
The implemented solver can solve QP with inequality constraints and with few modiﬁcations it
can solve equality constraints as well, however without any specializations. The methods proposed
by [28, chapter 3] add specializations to solving problems containing both equality and inequality
constraints. Exploring the beneﬁts of these methods would lead to interesting results and possible
faster execution.
Additional specializations can be made for simple bounds of the sort l ≤x ≤u, where l and
u are lower and upper bounds on the variable x. The specialization to this kind of bounds is
proposed by Jørgensen in [29]. This method applies the Cholescky factorization to a constructed
matrix and solving only part of the sub problem as the rest is redundant. This method reduces
the amount of calculations needed to ﬁnd the ﬁnal solution and will therefore be beneﬁcial. Many
more extensions exist and can also be investigated to reduce computation time, however when
applying specializations to the problem it is important to justify that the additional methods apply
to general problems or if the additions narrow the application of the solver too much.
11.3
Applying Other Methods for Solving
In Chapter 2 other methods are discussed to solve the general quadratic problem, including the
interior point algorithm and dual Active Set method. These methods all solve the general QP
85
==PAGE:112==
11.
Suggestions for Future Work
problem and therefore it is beneﬁcial to compare methods. Further also diﬀerent formulations of
the primal problem are beneﬁcial to benchmark and can include using l1 penalty terms to remove
the need for the phase one problem. This would remove the need for the phase one problem.
11.4
Programming Speciﬁc Optimizations
During the implementation of the solver a few code optimizations were made and surely a perfor-
mance gain can be made by optimizing all operations carried out during the Active Set method.
Additionally, by adding the possibility of parallel computation this would be beneﬁcial for the
general solver to lower running time, while retaining the platform independence. Parallelism is an
incredible opportunity to increase the eﬃciency of computations, even when considering platform
independence as many of of daily appliances have multiple processors, as both computers and any
mobile phones posses a muticored environment.
11.5
Benchmarking
Implementing the benchmarks that [32] propose will make the solver comparable to other current
implementations and test the solver against both bigger and more varied problems that mimic real
world applications such as portfolio optimization. This thesis used dense generated problems that
give an impression of the running time and handling of inequalities, however any improvements
done by future developments of the solver must also be compared to other solvers to reassure the
solvers performance. Completing a full benchmark test will also achieve a comparison to other
current solvers that are available, and allow the implementation to be rated accordingly.
